width="10"/></a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>International Red Cross and Red Crescent Movement</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Humanitarianism" title="Humanitarianism">humanitarian</a> movement with approximately 97 million <a href="/wiki/Volunteering" title="Volunteering">volunteers</a>, members and staff worldwide. It was founded to protect human life and health, to ensure respect for all human beings, and to prevent and alleviate human suffering. Within it there are three distinct organisations that are legally independent from each other, but are united within the movement through common basic principles, objectives, symbols, statutes and governing organisations.
</p>

<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation">Foundation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Foundation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sisters_of_Mercy_at_the_Battle_of_Gravelotte.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="702" data-file-width="880" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Sisters_of_Mercy_at_the_Battle_of_Gravelotte.jpg/170px-Sisters_of_Mercy_at_the_Battle_of_Gravelotte.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Sisters_of_Mercy_at_the_Battle_of_Gravelotte.jpg/255px-Sisters_of_Mercy_at_the_Battle_of_Gravelotte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Sisters_of_Mercy_at_the_Battle_of_Gravelotte.jpg/340px-Sisters_of_Mercy_at_the_Battle_of_Gravelotte.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sisters_of_Mercy_at_the_Battle_of_Gravelotte.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Red Cross, after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gravelotte" title="Battle of Gravelotte">Battle of Gravelotte</a> in 1870.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jean_Henri_Dunant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="819" data-file-width="630" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Jean_Henri_Dunant.jpg/170px-Jean_Henri_Dunant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Jean_Henri_Dunant.jpg/255px-Jean_Henri_Dunant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Jean_Henri_Dunant.jpg/340px-Jean_Henri_Dunant.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jean_Henri_Dunant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_Dunant" title="Henry Dunant">Henry Dunant</a>, author of <i><a href="/wiki/A_Memory_of_Solferino" title="A Memory of Solferino">A Memory of Solferino</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Until the middle of the nineteenth century, there were no organized or well-established army nursing systems for casualties, nor safe or protected institutions, to accommodate and treat those who were wounded on the battlefield. A devout <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a>, the Swiss businessman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean-Henri_Dunant" title="Jean-Henri Dunant">Jean-Henri Dunant</a> traveled to Italy to meet then-French emperor <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a> in June 1859 with the intention of discussing difficulties in conducting business in <a href="/wiki/French_Algeria" title="French Algeria">Algeria</a>, which at that time was occupied by <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoungHoyland2016354_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoungHoyland2016354-2">[2]</a></sup> He arrived in the small town of <a href="/wiki/Solferino" title="Solferino">Solferino</a> on the evening of 24 June after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Solferino" title="Battle of Solferino">Battle of Solferino</a>, an engagement in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Italian_War_of_Independence" title="Second Italian War of Independence">Austro-Sardinian War</a>. In a single day, about 40,000 soldiers on both sides died or were left wounded on the field. Dunant was shocked by the terrible aftermath of the battle, the suffering of the wounded soldiers, and the near-total lack of medical attendance and basic care. He completely abandoned the original intent of his trip and for several days he devoted himself to helping with the treatment and care for the wounded. He took point in organizing an overwhelming level of relief assistance with the local villagers to aid without discrimination.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="1882" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg/220px-Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg/330px-Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg/440px-Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Original document of the <a href="/wiki/First_Geneva_Convention" title="First Geneva Convention">First Geneva Convention</a>, 1864</div></div></div>
<p>Back at his home in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, he decided to write a book entitled <i><a href="/wiki/A_Memory_of_Solferino" title="A Memory of Solferino">A Memory of Solferino</a></i>, which he published using his own money in 1862. He sent copies of the book to leading political and military figures throughout Europe, and people he thought could help him make a change. His book included vivid descriptions of his experiences in Solferino in 1859, and he explicitly advocated the formation of national voluntary relief organizations to help nurse wounded soldiers in the case of war, inspired by Christian teaching regarding social responsibility and his experience after the battlefield of Solferino.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> He called for the development of an international treaty to guarantee the protection of medics and field hospitals for soldiers wounded on the battlefield.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cross_of_the_Serbian_Red_Cross_Society1876.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="827" data-file-width="827" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Cross_of_the_Serbian_Red_Cross_Society1876.jpg/220px-Cross_of_the_Serbian_Red_Cross_Society1876.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Cross_of_the_Serbian_Red_Cross_Society1876.jpg/330px-Cross_of_the_Serbian_Red_Cross_Society1876.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Cross_of_the_Serbian_Red_Cross_Society1876.jpg/440px-Cross_of_the_Serbian_Red_Cross_Society1876.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cross_of_the_Serbian_Red_Cross_Society1876.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross of the <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbian</a> Red Cross Society</div></div></div>
<p>In 1863, <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Moynier" title="Gustave Moynier">Gustave Moynier</a>, a Geneva lawyer and president of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Geneva_Society_for_Public_Welfare&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Geneva Society for Public Welfare (page does not exist)">Geneva Society for Public Welfare</a>, received a copy of Dunant's book and introduced it for discussion at a meeting of that society. As a result of this initial discussion, the society established an investigatory commission to examine the feasibility of Dunant's suggestions and eventually to organize an international conference about their possible implementation. The members of this committee, which has subsequently been referred to as the "Committee of the Five", aside from Dunant and Moynier were physician <a href="/wiki/Louis_Appia" title="Louis Appia">Louis Appia</a>, who had significant experience working as a field surgeon; Appia's friend and colleague <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9odore_Maunoir" title="Théodore Maunoir">Théodore Maunoir</a>, from the Geneva Hygiene and Health Commission; and <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Henri_Dufour" title="Guillaume Henri Dufour">Guillaume-Henri Dufour</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Armed_Forces" title="Swiss Armed Forces">Swiss army</a> general of great renown. Eight days later, the five men decided to rename the committee to the "International Committee for Relief to the Wounded".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_Conference">International Conference</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: International Conference">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From 26 to 29 October 1863,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunant18632_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunant18632-4">[4]</a></sup> the international conference organized by the committee was held in Geneva to develop possible measures to improve medical services on the battlefield. The conference was attended by 36 individuals: eighteen official delegates from national governments, six delegates from non-governmental organizations, seven non-official foreign delegates, and the five members of the International Committee. The states and kingdoms represented by official delegates were: <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austrian Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Baden" title="Grand Duchy of Baden">Grand Duchy of Baden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bavaria" title="Kingdom of Bavaria">Kingdom of Bavaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_French_Empire" title="Second French Empire">French Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hanover" title="Kingdom of Hanover">Kingdom of Hanover</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Hesse" title="Grand Duchy of Hesse">Grand Duchy of Hesse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Kingdom of Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Netherlands" title="Kingdom of the Netherlands">Kingdom of the Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Kingdom of Prussia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Saxony" title="Kingdom of Saxony">Kingdom of Saxony</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Spain_(1810%E2%80%9373)" title="History of Spain (1810–73)">Kingdom of Spain</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Kingdoms_of_Sweden_and_Norway" title="United Kingdoms of Sweden and Norway">United Kingdoms of Sweden and Norway</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBennett2006_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBennett2006-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Les_5_fondateurs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2785" data-file-width="3543" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Les_5_fondateurs.jpg/220px-Les_5_fondateurs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Les_5_fondateurs.jpg/330px-Les_5_fondateurs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Les_5_fondateurs.jpg/440px-Les_5_fondateurs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Les_5_fondateurs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Committee of the Five": Gustave Moynier, Guillaume-Henri Dufour, Henry Dunant, Louis Appia, Théodore Maunoir</div></div></div>
<p>Among the proposals written in the final resolutions of the conference, adopted on 29 October 1863,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> were:
</p>
<ul><li>The foundation of national relief societies for wounded soldiers;</li>
<li>Neutrality and protection for wounded soldiers;</li>
<li>The utilization of volunteer forces for relief assistance on the battlefield;</li>
<li>The organization of additional conferences to enact these concepts;</li>
<li>The introduction of a common distinctive protection symbol for medical personnel in the field, namely a white armlet bearing a red cross.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gedenkstein-rotes-kreuz-1864.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="621" data-file-width="492" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gedenkstein-rotes-kreuz-1864.jpg/220px-Gedenkstein-rotes-kreuz-1864.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gedenkstein-rotes-kreuz-1864.jpg/330px-Gedenkstein-rotes-kreuz-1864.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gedenkstein-rotes-kreuz-1864.jpg/440px-Gedenkstein-rotes-kreuz-1864.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gedenkstein-rotes-kreuz-1864.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Memorial commemorating the first use of the Red Cross symbol in an armed conflict during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dybb%C3%B8l" title="Battle of Dybbøl">Battle of Dybbøl</a> (Denmark) in 1864; jointly erected in 1989 by the national Red Cross societies of Denmark and Germany</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geneva_Convention">Geneva Convention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Geneva Convention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Only a year later, the Swiss government invited the governments of all European countries, as well as the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_(1849%E2%80%931865)" title="History of the United States (1849–1865)">United States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Brazil" title="Empire of Brazil">Empire of Brazil</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Second_Mexican_Empire" title="Second Mexican Empire">Mexican Empire</a> to attend an official diplomatic conference. Sixteen countries sent a total of twenty-six delegates to Geneva. On 22 August 1864, the conference adopted the first <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Conventions" title="Geneva Conventions">Geneva Convention</a> "for the Amelioration of the Condition of the Wounded in Armies in the Field". Representatives of 12 states and kingdoms signed the convention:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 22em;">
<ul><li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="17" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Switzerland.svg/17px-Flag_of_Switzerland.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Switzerland.svg/26px-Flag_of_Switzerland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Switzerland.svg/34px-Flag_of_Switzerland.svg.png 2x" width="17"/>  </span><a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss Confederation</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Flag_of_Baden_1862.svg/23px-Flag_of_Baden_1862.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Flag_of_Baden_1862.svg/35px-Flag_of_Baden_1862.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Flag_of_Baden_1862.svg/45px-Flag_of_Baden_1862.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Baden" title="Grand Duchy of Baden">Grand Duchy of Baden</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Flag_of_Belgium_%28civil%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_Belgium_%28civil%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Flag_of_Belgium_%28civil%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_Belgium_%28civil%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Flag_of_Belgium_%28civil%29.svg/45px-Flag_of_Belgium_%28civil%29.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Kingdom of Belgium</a></li>

<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Flag_of_France_%281794%E2%80%931815%2C_1830%E2%80%931974%2C_2020%E2%80%93present%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_France_%281794%E2%80%931815%2C_1830%E2%80%931974%2C_2020%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Flag_of_France_%281794%E2%80%931815%2C_1830%E2%80%931974%2C_2020%E2%80%93present%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_France_%281794%E2%80%931815%2C_1830%E2%80%931974%2C_2020%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Flag_of_France_%281794%E2%80%931815%2C_1830%E2%80%931974%2C_2020%E2%80%93present%29.svg/45px-Flag_of_France_%281794%E2%80%931815%2C_1830%E2%80%931974%2C_2020%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Second_French_Empire" title="Second French Empire">French Empire</a></li>

<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Flag_of_Italy_%281861-1946%29_crowned.svg/23px-Flag_of_Italy_%281861-1946%29_crowned.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Flag_of_Italy_%281861-1946%29_crowned.svg/35px-Flag_of_Italy_%281861-1946%29_crowned.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Flag_of_Italy_%281861-1946%29_crowned.svg/45px-Flag_of_Italy_%281861-1946%29_crowned.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Kingdom of Italy</a></li>

<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Flag_of_the_Kingdom_of_Prussia_%281803-1892%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_Kingdom_of_Prussia_%281803-1892%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Flag_of_the_Kingdom_of_Prussia_%281803-1892%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_Kingdom_of_Prussia_%281803-1892%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Flag_of_the_Kingdom_of_Prussia_%281803-1892%29.svg/45px-Flag_of_the_Kingdom_of_Prussia_%281803-1892%29.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Kingdom of Prussia</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Flagge_K%C3%B6nigreich_W%C3%BCrttemberg.svg/23px-Flagge_K%C3%B6nigreich_W%C3%BCrttemberg.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Flagge_K%C3%B6nigreich_W%C3%BCrttemberg.svg/35px-Flagge_K%C3%B6nigreich_W%C3%BCrttemberg.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Flagge_K%C3%B6nigreich_W%C3%BCrttemberg.svg/46px-Flagge_K%C3%B6nigreich_W%C3%BCrttemberg.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Kingdom of Württemberg">Kingdom of Württemberg</a></li></ul>
</div>
<p>The convention contained ten articles, establishing for the first time legally binding rules guaranteeing neutrality and protection for wounded soldiers, field medical personnel, and specific humanitarian institutions in an armed conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETelò2014_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETelò2014-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Directly following the establishment of the Geneva Convention, the first national societies were founded in Belgium, Denmark, France, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Oldenburg" title="Grand Duchy of Oldenburg">Oldenburg</a>, Prussia, Spain, and Württemberg. Also in 1864, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Appia" title="Louis Appia">Louis Appia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_William_Meredith_van_de_Velde" title="Charles William Meredith van de Velde">Charles van de Velde</a>, a captain of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Netherlands_Army" title="Royal Netherlands Army">Dutch Army</a>, became the first independent and neutral delegates to work under the symbol of the Red Cross in an armed conflict.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> government ratified this treaty on 5 July 1865. The <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Red_Crescent" title="Turkish Red Crescent">Turkish Red Crescent</a> organization was founded in the Ottoman Empire in 1868, partly in response to the experience of the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a> (1853-1856), in which disease overshadowed battle as the main cause of death and suffering among <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turkish</a> soldiers. It was the first Red Crescent society of its kind and one of the most important charity organizations in the <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslim</a> world.
</p><p>In 1867, the first International Conference of National Aid Societies for the Nursing of the War Wounded was convened.
Also in 1867, Jean-Henri Dunant was forced to declare <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy" title="Bankruptcy">bankruptcy</a> due to business failures in <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, partly because he had neglected his business interests during his tireless activities for the International Committee. The controversy surrounding Dunant's business dealings and the resulting negative public opinion, combined with an ongoing conflict with Gustave Moynier, led to Dunant's expulsion from his position as a member and secretary. He was charged with fraudulent bankruptcy and a warrant for his arrest was issued. Thus, he was forced to leave Geneva and never returned to his home city.
</p><p>In the following years, national societies were founded in nearly every country in Europe. The project resonated well with patriotic sentiments that were on the rise in the late-nineteenth-century, and national societies were often encouraged as signifiers of national moral superiority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDromi201679–97_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDromi201679–97-9">[9]</a></sup> In 1876, the committee adopted the name "International Committee of the Red Cross" (ICRC), which is still its official designation today. Five years later, the <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a> was founded through the efforts of <a href="/wiki/Clara_Barton" title="Clara Barton">Clara Barton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WDL_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-WDL-10">[10]</a></sup> More and more countries signed the Geneva Convention and began to respect it in practice during armed conflicts. In a rather short period of time, the Red Cross gained huge momentum as an internationally respected movement, and the national societies became increasingly popular as a venue for volunteer work.
</p><p>When the first <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> was awarded in 1901, the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Nobel_Committee" title="Norwegian Nobel Committee">Norwegian Nobel Committee</a> opted to give it jointly to Jean-Henri Dunant and <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Passy" title="Frédéric Passy">Frédéric Passy</a>, a leading international <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifist</a>. More significant than the honor of the prize itself, this prize marked the overdue rehabilitation of Jean-Henri Dunant and represented a tribute to his key role in the formation of the Red Cross. Dunant died nine years later in the small Swiss health resort of <a href="/wiki/Heiden,_Switzerland" title="Heiden, Switzerland">Heiden</a>. Only two months earlier his long-standing adversary Gustave Moynier had also died, leaving a mark in the history of the committee as its longest-serving president ever.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mikami_Red_Cross.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="631" data-file-width="840" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Mikami_Red_Cross.jpg/300px-Mikami_Red_Cross.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Mikami_Red_Cross.jpg/450px-Mikami_Red_Cross.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Mikami_Red_Cross.jpg/600px-Mikami_Red_Cross.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mikami_Red_Cross.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/G%C5%8Dtar%C5%8D_Mikami" title="Gōtarō Mikami">Gōtarō Mikami</a>'s Red Cross flag with which in 1905 he deflected from his field hospital in Manchuria the onslaught of the Russian army</div></div></div>
<p>In 1906, the 1864 Geneva Convention was revised for the first time. One year later, the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_of_1899_and_1907" title="Hague Conventions of 1899 and 1907">Hague Convention X</a>, adopted at the Second International Peace Conference in <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a>, extended the scope of the Geneva Convention to naval warfare. Shortly before the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a> in 1914, 50 years after the foundation of the ICRC and the adoption of the first Geneva Convention, there were already 45 national relief societies throughout the world. The movement had extended itself beyond Europe and North America to Central and South America (<a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentine Republic</a>, the <a href="/wiki/First_Brazilian_Republic" title="First Brazilian Republic">United States of Brazil</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Republic of Chile</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Republic of Cuba</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Mexican_States" title="United Mexican States">United Mexican States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Republic of Peru</a>, the <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">Republic of El Salvador</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Oriental Republic of Uruguay</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">United States of Venezuela</a>), Asia (the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">Republic of China</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Empire of Japan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Kingdom of Siam</a>), and Africa (<a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">Union of South Africa</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: World War I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:War_1914-1918._International_Prisoners-of-War_Agency.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2573" data-file-width="3593" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/War_1914-1918._International_Prisoners-of-War_Agency.jpg/220px-War_1914-1918._International_Prisoners-of-War_Agency.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/War_1914-1918._International_Prisoners-of-War_Agency.jpg/330px-War_1914-1918._International_Prisoners-of-War_Agency.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/War_1914-1918._International_Prisoners-of-War_Agency.jpg/440px-War_1914-1918._International_Prisoners-of-War_Agency.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:War_1914-1918._International_Prisoners-of-War_Agency.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>War 1914–1918. Geneva, <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Rath" title="Musée Rath">Rath Museum</a>. International Prisoners-of-War Agency. Researches department. German section. Express messages and communications to families.</div></div></div><p>
With the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the ICRC found itself confronted with enormous challenges that it could handle only by working closely with the national Red Cross societies. Red Cross nurses from around the world, including the United States and Japan, came to support the medical services of the armed forces of the European countries involved in the war. On 15 August 1914, immediately after the start of the war, the ICRC set up its International Prisoners-of-War Agency (IPWA) to trace <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">POWs</a> and to re-establish communications with their respective families. The <a href="/wiki/Austrians" title="Austrians">Austrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Writer" title="Writer">writer</a> and pacifist <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Zweig" title="Stefan Zweig">Stefan Zweig</a> described the situation at the Geneva headquarters of the ICRC:<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Hardly had the first blows been struck when cries of anguish from all lands began to be heard in Switzerland. Thousands who were without news of fathers, husbands, and sons in the battlefields, stretched despairing arms into the void. By hundreds, by thousands, by tens of thousands, letters and telegrams poured into the little House of the Red Cross in Geneva, the only international rallying point that still remained. Isolated, like stormy <a href="/wiki/Petrel" title="Petrel">petrels</a>, came the first inquiries for missing relatives; then these inquiries themselves became a storm. The letters arrived in sackfuls. Nothing had been prepared for dealing with such an inundation of misery. The Red Cross had no space, no organization, no system, and above all no helpers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZweig1921268_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZweig1921268-11">[11]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ICRC_V-P-HIST-01816-27.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3702" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/ICRC_V-P-HIST-01816-27.JPG/220px-ICRC_V-P-HIST-01816-27.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/ICRC_V-P-HIST-01816-27.JPG/330px-ICRC_V-P-HIST-01816-27.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/ICRC_V-P-HIST-01816-27.JPG/440px-ICRC_V-P-HIST-01816-27.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ICRC_V-P-HIST-01816-27.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Group picture of the volunteers – mostly women – in front of the Musée Rath in 1914</div></div></div><p>
However, by the end of the year, the Agency already had some 1,200 <a href="/wiki/Volunteering" title="Volunteering">volunteers</a> who worked in the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Rath" title="Musée Rath">Musée Rath</a> of Geneva, amongst them the <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> writer and pacifist <a href="/wiki/Romain_Rolland" title="Romain Rolland">Romain Rolland</a>. When he was awarded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_for_Literature" title="Nobel Prize for Literature">Nobel Prize for Literature</a> for 1915, he donated half of the <a href="/wiki/Prize_money" title="Prize money">prize money</a> to the Agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchazmann1955140–43_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchazmann1955140–43-12">[12]</a></sup> Most of the staff were women,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZweig1921268_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZweig1921268-11">[11]</a></sup> some of whom – like <a href="/wiki/Marguerite_Gautier-van_Berchem" title="Marguerite Gautier-van Berchem">Marguerite van Berchem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marguerite_Frick-Cramer" title="Marguerite Frick-Cramer">Marguerite Cramer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suzanne_Ferri%C3%A8re" title="Suzanne Ferrière">Suzanne Ferrière</a> – served in high positions as pioneers of <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a> in an organisation dominated by men.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_War_I_ambulance_Pittsburgh_20th_Century_Club.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="838" data-file-width="1190" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/World_War_I_ambulance_Pittsburgh_20th_Century_Club.png/220px-World_War_I_ambulance_Pittsburgh_20th_Century_Club.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/World_War_I_ambulance_Pittsburgh_20th_Century_Club.png/330px-World_War_I_ambulance_Pittsburgh_20th_Century_Club.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/World_War_I_ambulance_Pittsburgh_20th_Century_Club.png/440px-World_War_I_ambulance_Pittsburgh_20th_Century_Club.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_War_I_ambulance_Pittsburgh_20th_Century_Club.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Red Cross ambulance from 1917</div></div></div><p>By the end of the war, the Agency had transferred about 20 million letters and messages, 1.9 million parcels, and about 18 million <a href="/wiki/Swiss_franc" title="Swiss franc">Swiss francs</a> in monetary donations to POWs of all affected countries. Furthermore, due to the intervention of the Agency, about 200,000 prisoners were exchanged between the warring parties, released from captivity and returned to their home country. The organizational card index of the Agency accumulated about 7 million records from 1914 to 1923. The card index led to the identification of about 2 million POWs and the ability to contact their families. The complete index is on loan today from the ICRC to the <a href="/wiki/International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Museum" title="International Red Cross and Red Crescent Museum">International Red Cross and Red Crescent Museum</a> in Geneva. The right to access the index is still strictly restricted to the ICRC.
</p><p>During the entire war, the ICRC monitored warring parties' compliance with the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Conventions" title="Geneva Conventions">Geneva Conventions</a> of the 1907 revision and forwarded complaints about violations to the respective country. When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_weapons" title="Chemical weapons">chemical weapons</a> were used in this war for the first time in history, the ICRC mounted a vigorous protest against their use. Even without having a mandate from the Geneva Conventions, the ICRC tried to ameliorate the suffering of civil populations. In territories that were officially designated as "occupied territories", the ICRC could assist the civilian population on the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_of_1899_and_1907" title="Hague Conventions of 1899 and 1907">Hague Convention</a>'s "Laws and Customs of War on Land" of 1907.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> This convention was also the legal basis for the ICRC's work for prisoners of war. In addition to the work of the International Prisoner-of-War Agency as described above, this included inspection visits to POW camps. A total of 524 camps throughout Europe were visited by 41 delegates from the ICRC through the end of the war.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Under_the_Red_Cross_flag_at_home_and_abroad_(1915)_(14781982685).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="2159" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Under_the_Red_Cross_flag_at_home_and_abroad_%281915%29_%2814781982685%29.jpg/300px-Under_the_Red_Cross_flag_at_home_and_abroad_%281915%29_%2814781982685%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Under_the_Red_Cross_flag_at_home_and_abroad_%281915%29_%2814781982685%29.jpg/450px-Under_the_Red_Cross_flag_at_home_and_abroad_%281915%29_%2814781982685%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Under_the_Red_Cross_flag_at_home_and_abroad_%281915%29_%2814781982685%29.jpg/600px-Under_the_Red_Cross_flag_at_home_and_abroad_%281915%29_%2814781982685%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Under_the_Red_Cross_flag_at_home_and_abroad_(1915)_(14781982685).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The MV <i>Red Cross</i> in New York harbour ca 1915</div></div></div>
<p>Between 1916 and 1918, the ICRC published a number of <a href="/wiki/Postcard" title="Postcard">postcards</a> with scenes from the POW camps. The pictures showed the prisoners in day-to-day activities such as the distribution of letters from home. The intention of the ICRC was to provide the families of the prisoners with some hope and solace and to alleviate their uncertainties about the fate of their loved ones. After the end of the war, between 1920 and 1922, the ICRC organized the return of about 500,000 prisoners to their home countries. In 1920, the task of repatriation was handed over to the newly founded <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>, which appointed the Norwegian diplomat and scientist <a href="/wiki/Fridtjof_Nansen" title="Fridtjof Nansen">Fridtjof Nansen</a> as its "High Commissioner for Repatriation of the War Prisoners". His legal mandate was later extended to support and care for war refugees and displaced persons when his office became that of the League of Nations "High Commissioner for Refugees". Nansen, who invented the <a href="/wiki/Nansen_passport" title="Nansen passport">Nansen passport</a> for stateless refugees and was awarded the Nobel Peace Prize in 1922, appointed two delegates from the ICRC as his deputies.
A year before the end of the war, the ICRC received the 1917 Nobel Peace Prize for its outstanding wartime work. It was the only Nobel Peace Prize awarded in the period from 1914 to 1918. In 1923, the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a> adopted a change in its policy regarding the selection of new members. Until then, only citizens from the city of Geneva could serve in the committee. This limitation was expanded to include all Swiss citizens. As a direct consequence of World War I, a treaty was adopted in 1925 which outlawed the use of suffocating or poisonous gases and biological agents as weapons. Four years later, the original Convention was revised and the second Geneva Convention "relative to the Amelioration of the Condition of Wounded, Sick and Shipwrecked Members of Armed Forces at Sea" was established. The events of World War I and the respective activities of the ICRC significantly increased the reputation and authority of the Committee among the international community and led to an extension of its competencies.
</p><p>As early as in 1934, a draft proposal for an additional convention for the protection of the civil population in occupied territories during an armed conflict was adopted by the International Red Cross Conference. Unfortunately, most governments had little interest in implementing this convention, and it was thus prevented from entering into force before the beginning of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Theresienstadt_Ghetto_and_the_Red_Cross" title="Theresienstadt Ghetto and the Red Cross">Theresienstadt Ghetto and the Red Cross</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:War_Agency.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2535" data-file-width="3479" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/War_Agency.jpg/220px-War_Agency.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/War_Agency.jpg/330px-War_Agency.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/War_Agency.jpg/440px-War_Agency.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:War_Agency.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>War 1939–1945. Geneva, Central Prisoners of war Agency, Electoral building / Palace of the General Council</div></div></div>
<p>The Red Cross' response to the <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">Holocaust</a> has been the subject of significant controversy and criticism. As early as May 1944, the ICRC was criticized for its indifference to <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish</a> suffering and death—criticism that intensified after the end of the war, when the full extent of the Holocaust became undeniable. One defense to these allegations is that the Red Cross was trying to preserve its reputation as a neutral and impartial organization by not interfering with what was viewed as a German internal matter. The Red Cross also considered its primary focus to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners of war</a> whose countries had signed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geneva_Convention" title="Geneva Convention">Geneva Convention</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarréSchubert200972_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarréSchubert200972-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Children_playing_at_Theresienstadt_during_the_Red_Cross_visit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="236" data-file-width="421" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/eb/Children_playing_at_Theresienstadt_during_the_Red_Cross_visit.jpg/310px-Children_playing_at_Theresienstadt_during_the_Red_Cross_visit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/eb/Children_playing_at_Theresienstadt_during_the_Red_Cross_visit.jpg 1.5x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Children_playing_at_Theresienstadt_during_the_Red_Cross_visit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photo taken by Rossel at Theresienstadt. Most of the children were murdered at Auschwitz in the fall of 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The Geneva Conventions in their 1929 revision formed the legal basis of the work of the ICRC during World War II. The activities of the committee were similar to those during World War I: visiting and monitoring POW camps, organizing relief assistance for civilian populations, and administering the exchange of messages regarding prisoners and missing persons. By the end of the war, 179 delegates had conducted 12,750 visits to POW camps in 41 countries. The Central Information Agency on Prisoners-of-War (<i>Agence centrale des prisonniers de guerre</i>) had a staff of 3,000, the card index tracking prisoners contained 45 million cards, and 120 million messages were exchanged by the Agency. One major obstacle was that the <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazi</a>-controlled <a href="/wiki/German_Red_Cross" title="German Red Cross">German Red Cross</a> refused to cooperate with the Geneva statutes including blatant violations such as the deportation of Jews from Germany and the <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">mass murders</a> conducted in the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">Nazi concentration camps</a>. Moreover, two other main parties to the conflict, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and Japan, were not party to the 1929 Geneva Conventions and were not legally required to follow the rules of the conventions.
</p><p>During the war, the ICRC was unable to obtain an agreement with <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> about the treatment of detainees in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentration_camps" title="Concentration camps">concentration camps</a>, and it eventually abandoned applying pressure in order to avoid disrupting its work with POWs. The ICRC was also unable to obtain a response to reliable information about the <a href="/wiki/Extermination_camp" title="Extermination camp">extermination camps</a> and the mass killing of European Jews, <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Roma</a>, et al. After November 1943, the ICRC achieved permission to send <a href="/wiki/Red_Cross_parcel" title="Red Cross parcel">parcels</a> to concentration camp detainees with known names and locations. Because the notices of receipt for these parcels were often signed by other inmates, the ICRC managed to register the identities of about 105,000 detainees in the concentration camps and delivered about 1.1 million parcels, primarily to the camps <a href="/wiki/Dachau_concentration_camp" title="Dachau concentration camp">Dachau</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buchenwald" title="Buchenwald">Buchenwald</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ravensbr%C3%BCck" title="Ravensbrück">Ravensbrück</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sachsenhausen_concentration_camp" title="Sachsenhausen concentration camp">Sachsenhausen</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marcel_Junod-5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="589" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Marcel_Junod-5.jpg/220px-Marcel_Junod-5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Marcel_Junod-5.jpg/330px-Marcel_Junod-5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Marcel_Junod-5.jpg/440px-Marcel_Junod-5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marcel_Junod-5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marcel_Junod" title="Marcel Junod">Marcel Junod</a>, delegate of the ICRC, visiting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_War" title="Prisoner of War">POWs</a> in Germany</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Maurice_Rossel" title="Maurice Rossel">Maurice Rossel</a> was sent to Berlin as a delegate of the International Red Cross; he visited <a href="/wiki/Theresienstadt_Ghetto" title="Theresienstadt Ghetto">Theresienstadt</a> in 1944. The choice of the inexperienced Rossel for this mission has been interpreted as indicative of his organization's indifference to the "Jewish problem", while his report has been described as "emblematic of the failure of the ICRC" to advocate for Jews during the Holocaust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFarréSchubert2009abstract_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFarréSchubert2009abstract-16">[16]</a></sup> Rossel's report was noted for its uncritical acceptance of <a href="/wiki/Propaganda_in_Nazi_Germany" title="Propaganda in Nazi Germany">Nazi propaganda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[a]</a></sup> He erroneously stated that Jews were not deported from Theresienstadt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothkirchen2006258_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothkirchen2006258-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Claude_Lanzmann" title="Claude Lanzmann">Claude Lanzmann</a> recorded his experiences in 1979, producing a documentary entitled <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Visitor_from_the_Living" title="A Visitor from the Living">A Visitor from the Living</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ICRC-Archives_FritzBilfinger-TelegramFromHiroshima-30August1945.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2784" data-file-width="5976" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/ICRC-Archives_FritzBilfinger-TelegramFromHiroshima-30August1945.jpg/220px-ICRC-Archives_FritzBilfinger-TelegramFromHiroshima-30August1945.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/ICRC-Archives_FritzBilfinger-TelegramFromHiroshima-30August1945.jpg/330px-ICRC-Archives_FritzBilfinger-TelegramFromHiroshima-30August1945.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/ICRC-Archives_FritzBilfinger-TelegramFromHiroshima-30August1945.jpg/440px-ICRC-Archives_FritzBilfinger-TelegramFromHiroshima-30August1945.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ICRC-Archives_FritzBilfinger-TelegramFromHiroshima-30August1945.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Telegram by ICRC delegate <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fritz_Bilfinger&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fritz Bilfinger (page does not exist)">Fritz Bilfinger</a> from <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a> three weeks after the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombing</a></div></div></div>
<p>On 12 March 1945, ICRC president <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jacob_Burckhardt_(Red_Cross)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jacob Burckhardt (Red Cross) (page does not exist)">Jacob Burckhardt</a> received a message from SS General <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Kaltenbrunner" title="Ernst Kaltenbrunner">Ernst Kaltenbrunner</a> allowing ICRC delegates to visit the concentration camps. This agreement was bound by the condition that these delegates would have to stay in the camps until the end of the war. Ten delegates, among them <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Louis_Haefliger&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Louis Haefliger (page does not exist)">Louis Haefliger</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauthausen-Gusen" title="Mauthausen-Gusen">Mauthausen-Gusen</a>), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Paul_Dunant&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Paul Dunant (page does not exist)">Paul Dunant</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theresienstadt" title="Theresienstadt">Theresienstadt</a>) and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Victor_Maurer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Victor Maurer (page does not exist)">Victor Maurer</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dachau_concentration_camp" title="Dachau concentration camp">Dachau</a>), accepted the assignment and visited the camps. Louis Haefliger prevented the forceful eviction or blasting of Mauthausen-Gusen by alerting American troops.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Born" title="Friedrich Born">Friedrich Born</a> (1903–1963), an ICRC delegate in <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a> who saved the lives of about 11,000 to 15,000 Jewish people in <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>. <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Junod" title="Marcel Junod">Marcel Junod</a> (1904–1961), a physician from Geneva was one of the first foreigners to visit <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Little_Boy" title="Little Boy">atomic bomb</a> was dropped.
</p><p>In 1944, the ICRC received its second Nobel Peace Prize. As in World War I, it received the only Peace Prize awarded during the main period of war, 1939 to 1945. At the end of the war, the ICRC worked with national Red Cross societies to organize relief assistance to those countries most severely affected. In 1948, the Committee published a report reviewing its war-era activities from 1 September 1939 to 30 June 1947. The ICRC opened its archives from World War II in 1996.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_World_War_II">After World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: After World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PoW_1945.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2339" data-file-width="3500" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/PoW_1945.jpg/220px-PoW_1945.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/PoW_1945.jpg/330px-PoW_1945.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/PoW_1945.jpg/440px-PoW_1945.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PoW_1945.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Budapest 1945. Repatriation of 2000 Italian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners of war</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>On 12 August 1949, further revisions to the existing two Geneva Conventions were adopted. An additional convention "for the Amelioration of the Condition of Wounded, Sick and Shipwrecked Members of Armed Forces at Sea", now called the second Geneva Convention, was brought under the Geneva Convention umbrella as a successor to the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_of_1899_and_1907" title="Hague Conventions of 1899 and 1907">1907 Hague Convention X</a>. The 1929 Geneva convention "relative to the Treatment of Prisoners of War" may have been the second Geneva Convention from a historical point of view (because it was actually formulated in Geneva), but after 1949 it came to be called the third Convention because it came later chronologically than the Hague Convention. Reacting to the experience of World War II, the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Geneva_Convention" title="Fourth Geneva Convention">Fourth Geneva Convention</a>, a new Convention "relative to the Protection of Civilian Persons in Time of War", was established. Also, the additional protocols of 8 June 1977 were intended to make the conventions apply to internal conflicts such as civil wars. Today, the four conventions and their added protocols contain more than 600 articles, a remarkable expansion when compared to the mere 10 articles in the first 1864 convention.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross,_Geneva_(46907115094).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3783" data-file-width="5700" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross%2C_Geneva_%2846907115094%29.jpg/220px-International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross%2C_Geneva_%2846907115094%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross%2C_Geneva_%2846907115094%29.jpg/330px-International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross%2C_Geneva_%2846907115094%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross%2C_Geneva_%2846907115094%29.jpg/440px-International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross%2C_Geneva_%2846907115094%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross,_Geneva_(46907115094).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ICRC Headquarters in Geneva</div></div></div>
<p>In celebration of its centennial in 1963, the ICRC, together with the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_of_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Societies" title="International Federation of Red Cross and Red Crescent Societies">League of Red Cross Societies</a>, received its third Nobel Peace Prize. Since 1993, non-Swiss individuals have been allowed to serve as Committee delegates abroad, a task which was previously restricted to Swiss citizens. Indeed, since then, the share of staff without Swiss citizenship has increased to about 35%.
</p><p>On 16 October 1990, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_General_Assembly" title="UN General Assembly">UN General Assembly</a> decided to grant the ICRC <a href="/wiki/Observer_status" title="Observer status">observer status</a> for its assembly sessions and sub-committee meetings, the first observer status given to a private organization. The resolution was jointly proposed by 138 member states and introduced by the <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italian</a> ambassador, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Vieri_Traxler&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Vieri Traxler (page does not exist)">Vieri Traxler</a>, in memory of the organization's origins in the Battle of Solferino. An agreement with the Swiss government signed on 19 March 1993, affirmed the already long-standing policy of full independence of the committee from any possible interference by Switzerland. The agreement protects the full sanctity of all ICRC property in Switzerland including its headquarters and archive, grants members and staff legal immunity, exempts the ICRC from all taxes and fees, guarantees the protected and duty-free transfer of goods, services, and money, provides the ICRC with secure communication privileges at the same level as foreign embassies, and simplifies Committee travel in and out of Switzerland.
</p><p>At the end of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, the ICRC's work actually became more dangerous. In the 1990s, more delegates died than at any point in its history, especially when working in local and internal armed conflicts. These incidents often demonstrated a lack of respect for the rules of the Geneva Conventions and their protection symbols. Among the slain delegates were:
</p>
<ul><li>Frédéric Maurice. He died on 19 May 1992 at the age of 39, one day after a Red Cross transport he was escorting was attacked in the <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a>.</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fernanda_Calado&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fernanda Calado (page does not exist)">Fernanda Calado</a> (Spain), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ingeborg_Foss&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ingeborg Foss (page does not exist)">Ingeborg Foss</a> (Norway), <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Malloy" title="Nancy Malloy">Nancy Malloy</a> (Canada), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gunnhild_Myklebust&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gunnhild Myklebust (page does not exist)">Gunnhild Myklebust</a> (Norway), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sheryl_Thayer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sheryl Thayer (page does not exist)">Sheryl Thayer</a> (<a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>), and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hans_Elkerbout&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hans Elkerbout (page does not exist)">Hans Elkerbout</a> (Netherlands). They were shot at point-blank range while sleeping in the early hours of 17 December 1996 in the ICRC field hospital in the <a href="/wiki/Chechnya" title="Chechnya">Chechen</a> city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nowije_Atagi" title="Nowije Atagi">Nowije Atagi</a> near <a href="/wiki/Grozny" title="Grozny">Grozny</a>. Their murderers have never been caught and there was no apparent motive for the killings.</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rita_Fox&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rita Fox (page does not exist)">Rita Fox</a> (Switzerland), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=V%C3%A9ronique_Saro&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Véronique Saro (page does not exist)">Véronique Saro</a> (<a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, formerly known as Zaire), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Julio_Delgado&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Julio Delgado (page does not exist)">Julio Delgado</a> (<a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Unen_Ufoirworth&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Unen Ufoirworth (page does not exist)">Unen Ufoirworth</a> (DR Congo), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aduwe_Boboli&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aduwe Boboli (page does not exist)">Aduwe Boboli</a> (DR Congo), and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jean_Molokabonge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jean Molokabonge (page does not exist)">Jean Molokabonge</a> (DR Congo). On 26 April 2001, they were en route with two cars on a relief mission in the northeast of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> when they came under fatal fire from unknown attackers.</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ricardo_Mungu%C3%ADa_(aid_worker)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ricardo Munguía (aid worker) (page does not exist)">Ricardo Munguia</a> (El Salvador). He was working as a water engineer in Afghanistan and travelling with local colleagues on 27 March 2003 when their car was stopped by unknown armed men. He was shot execution-style while his colleagues were allowed to escape. He was 39 years old. The killing prompted the ICRC to temporarily suspend operations across Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vatche_Arslanian" title="Vatche Arslanian">Vatche Arslanian</a> (Canada). Since 2001, he worked as a logistics coordinator for the ICRC mission in Iraq. He died when he was travelling through Baghdad together with members of the Iraqi Red Crescent. On 8 April 2003 their car accidentally came into the cross fire of fighting in the city.</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nadisha_Yasassri_Ranmuthu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nadisha Yasassri Ranmuthu (page does not exist)">Nadisha Yasassri Ranmuthu</a> (Sri Lanka). He was killed by unknown attackers on 22 July 2003 when his car was fired upon near the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hilla" title="Hilla">Hilla</a> in the south of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>.</li></ul>
<p>In the 2000s, the ICRC has been active in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">Afghanistan conflict</a> areas and has set up six physical rehabilitation centers to help <a href="/wiki/Land_mine" title="Land mine">land mine</a> victims. Their support extends to the national and international armed forces, civilians and the armed opposition. They regularly visit detainees under the custody of the Afghan government and the international armed forces, but have also occasionally had access since 2009 to people detained by the <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> They have provided basic first aid training and aid kits to both the Afghan security forces and Taliban members because, according to an ICRC spokesperson, "ICRC's constitution stipulates that all parties harmed by warfare will be treated as fairly as possible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> In August 2021, when <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>-led forces retreated from Afghanistan, the ICRC decided to remain in the country to continue its mission to assist and protect victims of conflict. Since June 2021, ICRC-supported facilities have treated more than 40,000 people wounded during armed confrontations there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the ten largest ICRC deployments worldwide has been the mission in <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, where the organisation has been active since 2014, working closely with the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Red_Cross_Society" title="Ukrainian Red Cross Society">Ukrainian Red Cross Society</a>. At first, the ICRC was active primarily in the disputed regions of <a href="/wiki/Donbas" title="Donbas">Donbas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Donetsk" title="Donetsk">Donetsk</a>, assisting persons injured by armed confrontations there. When Russia invaded Ukraine on 24 February 2022, the fighting moved to more populated areas in the Eastern, North and South Ukraine. The head of the ICRC delegation in <a href="/wiki/Kyiv" title="Kyiv">Kyiv</a> warned on 26 February 2022 that neighborhoods of major cities are becoming the frontline with significant consequences for their populations, including children, the sick and elderly. The ICRC had urgently called on all parties to the conflict not to forget their obligations under <a href="/wiki/International_humanitarian_law" title="International humanitarian law">international humanitarian law</a> to ensure the protection of the civilian population and infrastructure, and respect the dignity of refugees and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners of war</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="IFRC">IFRC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: IFRC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Davison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="711" data-file-width="497" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Henry_Davison.jpg/170px-Henry_Davison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Henry_Davison.jpg/255px-Henry_Davison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Henry_Davison.jpg/340px-Henry_Davison.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Davison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_Pomeroy_Davison" title="Henry Pomeroy Davison">Henry Davison</a>, Founding father of the League of Red Cross societies</div></div></div>
<p>In 1919, representatives from the national Red Cross societies of Britain, France, Italy, Japan, and the US came together in Paris to found the "League of Red Cross Societies" (IFRC). The original idea came from <a href="/wiki/Henry_Pomeroy_Davison" title="Henry Pomeroy Davison">Henry Davison</a>, who was then president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a>. This move, led by the American Red Cross, expanded the international activities of the Red Cross movement beyond the strict mission of the ICRC to include relief assistance in response to emergency situations which were not caused by war (such as man-made or natural disasters). The ARC already had great disaster relief mission experience extending back to its foundation.
</p><p>The formation of the League, as an additional international Red Cross organization alongside the ICRC, was not without controversy for a number of reasons. The ICRC had, to some extent, valid concerns about a possible rivalry between the two organizations. The foundation of the League was seen as an attempt to undermine the leadership position of the ICRC within the movement and to gradually transfer most of its tasks and competencies to a multilateral institution. In addition to that, all founding members of the League were national societies from countries of the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Entente" title="Triple Entente">Entente</a> or from associated partners of the Entente.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDromi2020119_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDromi2020119-26">[25]</a></sup> The original statutes of the League from May 1919 contained further regulations which gave the five founding societies a privileged status and, due to the efforts of Henry Davison, the right to permanently exclude the national Red Cross societies from the countries of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a>, namely Germany, Austria, Hungary, Bulgaria and Turkey, and in addition to that the national Red Cross society of Russia. These rules were contrary to the Red Cross principles of universality and equality among all national societies, a situation which furthered the concerns of the ICRC.
</p><p>The first relief assistance mission organized by the League was an aid mission for the victims of a famine and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a> epidemic in Poland. Only five years after its foundation, the League had already issued 47 donation appeals for missions in 34 countries, an impressive indication of the need for this type of Red Cross work. The total sum raised by these appeals reached 685 million Swiss francs, which were used to bring emergency supplies to the victims of famines in Russia, Germany, and Albania; earthquakes in Chile, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Persia</a>, Japan, Colombia, Ecuador, Costa Rica, and Turkey; and refugee flows in Greece and Turkey. The first large-scale disaster mission of the League came after the 1923 earthquake in Japan which killed about 200,000 people and left countless more wounded and without shelter. Due to the League's coordination, the Red Cross society of Japan received goods from its sister societies reaching a total worth of about $100 million. Another important new field initiated by the League was the creation of youth Red Cross organizations within the national societies.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_stamp_384_Red_Cross.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Faroe_stamp_384_Red_Cross.jpg/220px-Faroe_stamp_384_Red_Cross.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/Faroe_stamp_384_Red_Cross.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_stamp_384_Red_Cross.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A stamp from the Faroe Islands</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Timbre_Turquie_Croissant_rouge_1928.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="706" data-file-width="948" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Timbre_Turquie_Croissant_rouge_1928.jpg/220px-Timbre_Turquie_Croissant_rouge_1928.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Timbre_Turquie_Croissant_rouge_1928.jpg/330px-Timbre_Turquie_Croissant_rouge_1928.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Timbre_Turquie_Croissant_rouge_1928.jpg/440px-Timbre_Turquie_Croissant_rouge_1928.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Timbre_Turquie_Croissant_rouge_1928.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A stamp from Turkey</div></div></div>
<p>A joint mission of the ICRC and the League in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Civil_War" title="Russian Civil War">Russian Civil War</a> from 1917 to 1922 marked the first time the movement was involved in an internal conflict, although still without an explicit mandate from the Geneva Conventions. The League, with support from more than 25 national societies, organized assistance missions and the distribution of food and other aid goods for civil populations affected by hunger and disease. The ICRC worked with the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Red_Cross_Society" title="Russian Red Cross Society">Russian Red Cross Society</a> and later the society of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, constantly emphasizing the ICRC's neutrality. In 1928, the "International Council" was founded to coordinate cooperation between the ICRC and the League, a task which was later taken over by the "Standing Commission". In the same year, a common statute for the movement was adopted for the first time, defining the respective roles of the ICRC and the League within the movement.
</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Abyssinian_War" title="Second Italo-Abyssinian War">Abyssinian war</a> between Ethiopia and Italy from 1935 to 1936, the League contributed aid supplies worth about 1.7 million Swiss francs. Because the Italian fascist regime under <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> refused any cooperation with the Red Cross, these goods were delivered solely to Ethiopia. During the war, an estimated 29 people died while being under explicit protection of the Red Cross symbol, most of them due to attacks by the Italian Army. During the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">civil war in Spain</a> from 1936 to 1939 the League once again joined forces with the ICRC with the support of 41 national societies. In 1939 on the brink of the Second World War, the League relocated its headquarters from Paris to Geneva to take advantage of Swiss neutrality.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friedensnobelpreis-1963.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="531" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Friedensnobelpreis-1963.jpg/220px-Friedensnobelpreis-1963.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Friedensnobelpreis-1963.jpg/330px-Friedensnobelpreis-1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Friedensnobelpreis-1963.jpg/440px-Friedensnobelpreis-1963.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friedensnobelpreis-1963.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Peace Nobel Prize ceremony in 1963. From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Harald_V_of_Norway" title="Harald V of Norway">Crown Prince Harald of Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olav_V_of_Norway" title="Olav V of Norway">King Olav of Norway</a>, ICRC president Leopold Boissier, League Chairman John A. MacAulay.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1952, the 1928 common statute of the movement was revised for the first time. Also, the period of <a href="/wiki/Decolonization" title="Decolonization">decolonization</a> from 1960 to 1970 was marked by a huge jump in the number of recognized national Red Cross and Red Crescent societies. By the end of the 1960s, there were more than 100 societies around the world. On 10 December 1963, the Federation and the ICRC received the Nobel Peace Prize. In 1983, the League was renamed to the "League of Red Cross and Red Crescent Societies" to reflect the growing number of national societies operating under the Red Crescent symbol. Three years later, the seven basic principles of the movement as adopted in 1965 were incorporated into its statutes. The name of the League was changed again in 1991 to its current official designation the "International Federation of Red Cross and Red Crescent Societies". In 1997, the ICRC and the IFRC signed the <a href="/wiki/Seville_Agreement" title="Seville Agreement">Seville Agreement</a> which further defined the responsibilities of both organizations within the movement. In 2004, the IFRC began its largest mission to date after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2004_Indian_Ocean_earthquake" title="2004 Indian Ocean earthquake">tsunami disaster in South Asia</a>. More than 40 national societies have worked with more than 22,000 volunteers to bring relief to the countless victims left without food and shelter and endangered by the risk of epidemics.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Activities">Activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Activities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
